Introduction to the Book of Divorce: Talaq al-Sunnah
Chapter heading introducing talaq al-sunnah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the structure of the Hanafi fiqh section on divorce and define talaq al-sunnah as the recommended divorce method.

Core Idea
Talaq al-sunnah is the recommended divorce method; other forms are valid but sinful.

We begin the Book of Divorce (Kitab al-Talaq) with its first chapter, 'Divorce According to Sunnah' (Talaq al-Sunnah). In Hanafi fiqh, divorce is permissible but strongly discouraged; however, if it must occur, it should follow the prophetic guidance. This lesson anchors the terminology and structure, emphasizing that talaq al-sunnah is the correct method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the text's structure
The passage is a chapter heading: 'Book of Divorce, Chapter on Divorce According to Sunnah'. Recognize that this introduces a major section of fiqh.
The Hidayah organizes fiqh topically; this is the start of a new topic.
2
Recall the key term
Talaq al-sunnah refers to a single revocable divorce pronounced during the wife's pure state without intercourse.
This is the recommended form; other forms like triple divorce in one sitting are sinful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق
باب طلاق السنة
Book of Divorce. Chapter on Divorce According to Sunnah.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This is a front matter entry indicating the beginning of the Book of Divorce (talaq) and its first chapter on divorce according to the Sunnah (talaq al-sunnah). In Hanafi fiqh, talaq al-sunnah refers to the correct method of divorce that complies with the prophetic guidance: a single revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) pronounced during the wife's purity (tuhr) without intercourse. The chapter will detail the conditions, recommended practices, and rulings. This is a memorization anchor for students to recall the structure of the Hidayah's chapters on divorce.

Key Terms
طلاق السنة (talaq al-sunnah): Divorce in accordance with prophetic sunnah, i.e., one pronouncement during the wife's clean period without intercourse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first chapter of the Book of Divorce in this text?
A) باب طلاق السنة
B) باب الرجعة
C) باب الخلع
D) باب الظهار

Key Takeaway
Talaq al-sunnah is the recommended divorce method in Hanafi fiqh, defined by specific conditions.

Divorce by Similitude and Descriptive Attributes
Rulings on divorce with gesture and attributes of severity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a gesture indicating the number of divorces and a descriptive attribute that does not increase the count, and state the ruling for each scenario.

Core Idea
A gesture with three fingers gives three divorces; words describing severity give only one — description enhances quality, not quantity.

In this passage, we explore two ways a husband might accompany his divorce pronouncement: a physical gesture indicating the number of divorces, or a verbal description emphasizing severity. Both affect the outcome differently. Understanding this distinction prevents counting errors and ensures correct application of talaq rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
The act is the husband saying 'anti taliq' (you are divorced) with an additional element: either a hand gesture or a descriptive word.
The base pronouncement is talaq.
2
Classify the additional element
Determine if the element is a physical gesture (like showing fingers) that implies a number, or a verbal description (like 'severest') that describes quality.
Gesture is visual; description is spoken.
3
Apply the rule for gestures
In Hanafi law, gestures with clear customary meaning count. Showing thumb, index, and middle finger together indicates three divorces.
Common practice: three fingers = three.
4
Apply the rule for descriptions
Descriptive words like 'afḥash al-ṭalāq' (most obscene), 'kal-jabal' (like a mountain), or 'ṭalqatan shadīdah' (severe divorce) do not increase the number. Only one divorce occurs, and it is bā'in (irrevocable).
The description qualifies the divorce; it does not multiply it.
5
State the final ruling
Gesture with three fingers → three divorces. Any descriptive attribute → one bā'in divorce.
The same applies to all given examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تشبیہ طلاق اور اس کے وصف کے بیان میں انت طالق هكذا كبہ کر انگوٹھے ،سابہ اور وسطی سے اشارہ کیا تو تین طلاقیں واقع ہوں گی طلاق کو شدت یا زیادہ کے وصف کے ساتھ متصف کیا تو کتنی طلاقیں واقع ہوں گی انت طالق افحش الطلاق سے طلاق دینے کا حکم انت طالق کالجبل سے طلاق دینے کا حکم انت طالق اشد الطلاق او كالف او من البیت سے کتنی طلاقیں واقع ہوں گی انت طالق تطليقه شديدة او عريضة او طويلة ایک طلاق بائنہ واقع ہوگی
Regarding similitude divorce and its description: if a man says 'anti taliq hākadhā' and gestures with thumb, index[middle?] and middle finger, three divorces occur. If divorce is described with an attribute of severity or excess, how many divorces occur? Ruling on giving divorce with 'anti taliq afḥash al-ṭalāq'. Ruling on giving divorce with 'anti taliq kal-jabal'. With 'anti taliq ashadd al-ṭalāq' or 'kalif' or 'min al-bayt', how many divorces occur? 'Anti taliq ṭalqatan shadīdatan aw ‘arīḍatan aw ṭawīlatan' – one bā'in divorce occurs.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah covers the rulings of tashbih (similitude) divorce and describing divorce with attributes. When a man says 'anti taliq' and uses a hand gesture indicating three (thumb, index, and middle finger), three divorces are effected because the gesture is a clear indication. However, if he describes the divorce with attributes of severity or exaggeration—such as 'afḥash al-ṭalāq' (most obscene divorce), 'kal-jabal' (like a mountain), 'ashadd al-ṭalāq' (most severe), 'kalif' (like a burden?), or 'min al-bayt' (from the house)—these attributes do not increase the number; only one bā'in divorce occurs. Similarly, describing a single divorce as 'severe', 'wide', or 'long' still results in one bā'in divorce. The key principle is that the description pertains to the quality, not the quantity.

Key Terms
تشبیہ طلاق (tashbīh al-ṭalāq): Divorce by similitude, where a gesture or description implies the number of divorces.
بائنہ (bā'inah): A divorce that immediately severs the marital bond, allowing no revocation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says 'anti taliq ṭalqatan shadīdatan aw ‘arīḍatan aw ṭawīlatan', how many divorces occur?
A) Three
B) One bā'in
C) One raj'ī
D) It depends on the court

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a gesture with three fingers gives three divorces; any descriptive attribute, no matter how strong, gives only one bā'in divorce.

Divorce Before Consummation: Three Separate Talaq
Ruling on three separate divorces before consummation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on three separate divorces given before consummation and explain why only the first is effective.

Core Idea
A single divorce before consummation is bā'in; the marriage ends immediately, so subsequent divorces are void.

This lesson focuses on the Hanafi position regarding divorce before physical consummation (dukhūl). When a husband pronounces three separate divorces before the marriage has been consummated, only the first takes effect, as it severs the marital bond irrevocably. Understanding this rule prevents confusion about the status of further pronouncements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and its key element
The husband gives three separate talaq before consummation. Note the absence of dukhūl.
2
Determine the effect of the first divorce
The first talaq makes the wife bā'in (irrevocably divorced). The marriage ends immediately.
3
Understand the status of further divorces
Since the marriage has ended, the second and third pronouncements have no legal effect; they are not valid talaq.
4
Contrast with divorce after consummation
After consummation, the first two divorces are raj'ī (revocable) and only the third becomes bā'in. This highlights the unique rule for pre-consummation talaq.
This step provides context but is not explicitly in the source text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دخول سے پہلے طلاق دینے کا بیان .. دخول سے پہلے تین طلاق دینے کا حکم اگر جدا تین طلاقین دیں تو پہلی سے بائنہ ہو جائے گی
Statement on giving divorce before consummation.. Ruling on giving three divorces before consummation: if three divorces are given separately, she becomes bā'in by the first.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling when a husband divorces his wife before consummation (dukhūl) and gives three divorces separately, not all at once. According to the Hanafi madhhab, before consummation, a single divorce is bā'in (irrevocable). Therefore, if he says 'anti ṭāliq' once, she becomes bā'in, and subsequent attempts to divorce are ineffective because the marriage is already severed. The first divorce takes effect immediately, and the second and third are not valid.

Key Terms
دخول (dukhūl): Consummation of marriage, or physical seclusion that makes divorce raj'ī.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man gives three divorces separately before consummation, how many are effective?
A) Three
B) Two
C) One (bā'in)
D) None

Key Takeaway
Before consummation, a single separate divorce is bā'in and ends the marriage immediately; subsequent pronouncements are void.

Additive and Sequential Divorce Phrases
Ruling on divorce phrases with conjunctions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when the phrase 'wahidatan wa wahidatan' gives one divorce versus two, and apply the rule for 'qabla/ba‘d' and 'ma‘a'.

Core Idea
Conjunctions matter: 'wa' gives one, 'qabla/ba‘d' gives one, 'ma‘a' gives two.

This passage clarifies how the choice of conjunction in a divorce pronouncement affects the number of divorces that occur. Although a husband may say 'one and one', the Hanafi school interprets 'wa' as emphasis unless a separate intention is proven. However, using 'ma‘a' (with) explicitly indicates two separate divorces.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of phrases
Recognize the three constructions: 'wahidatan wa wahidatan', 'qabla wahidatin / ba‘daha wahidatan', and 'ma‘a wahidatin / ma‘aha wahidatan'.
2
Differentiate the conjunctions
Note that 'wa' and 'qabla/ba‘d' imply sequence or emphasis, thus one divorce; 'ma‘a' implies simultaneity and separate action, thus two.
The reasoning is based on the literal meaning of the conjunctions.
3
Apply to a new example
Given a phrase, determine the number of divorces by identifying the conjunction and applying the rule.
Practice with phrases like 'anti taliq wahidatan wa wahidatan'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انت طالق واحدة وواحدة سے طلاق دینے کا حکم انت طالق واحدة قبل واحدة او بعدها واحدۃ سے ایک طلاق واقع ہوگی انت طالق واحدة مع واحدة او معها واحدۃ سے دو طلاقیں واقع ہوں گی
Ruling on giving divorce with 'anti taliq wahidatan wa wahidatan'. With 'anti taliq wahidatan qabla wahidatin aw ba‘daha wahidatan' one divorce occurs. With 'anti taliq wahidatan ma‘a wahidatin aw ma‘aha wahidatan' two divorces occur.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage explains how differing conjunctions change the number of divorces. If a man says 'anti taliq wahidatan wa wahidatan' (you are divorced once and once), the implication is that he says 'one and one', but the 'wa' (and) can be interpreted as emphasis or addition. According to Hanafi, if he says 'wahidatan qabla wahidatin' (one before one) or 'ba‘daha wahidatan' (after her, one), it is considered a single divorce—the second 'one' is redundant. However, if he uses 'ma‘a' (with) or 'ma‘aha' (along with her), such as 'ma‘a wahidatin' or 'ma‘aha wahidatan', it indicates two separate divorces, so two occur.

Key Terms
واحدة وواحدة (wāḥidatun wa wāḥidatun): Phrase 'one and one' – interpreted as repetition of the same divorce unless specified otherwise.
قبل (qabla): before – indicates sequence, thus the second 'one' is redundant.
بعدها (ba‘daha): after her – similar to qabla, indicates sequence.
مع (ma‘a): with – indicates accompaniment, thus the second 'one' is a separate divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says 'anti taliq wahidatan ma‘a wahidatin'. How many divorces occur?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) None

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi talaq, conjunctions determine the number: 'wa', 'qabla', 'ba‘d' give one; 'ma‘a' gives two.

Conditional Divorce with 'wa wahidatan': One or Two?
Two opinions on conditional 'one and one'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi opinions on whether 'wāḥidatan wa wāḥidatan' in a conditional divorce yields one or two divorces and explain the legal reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
In conditional divorce, 'one and one' may be one or two—the phrasing is ambiguous.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in Hanafi divorce law: when a husband conditions talaq on an event and uses the phrase 'one and one'. The classical Hanafi jurists differed on whether this results in a single divorce or two. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens our appreciation of how subtle linguistic nuances can affect legal outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional phrase
The husband says: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced once and once.' The condition is entering the house; the consequence is talaq with the wording 'wāḥidatan wa wāḥidatan'.
The phrase is conditional, not immediate.
2
Recognize the two interpretive possibilities
One opinion treats the second 'once' as emphasis, so only one divorce occurs. Another opinion treats it as a separate declaration, so two divorces occur when the condition is fulfilled.
Both opinions are within the Hanafi school.
3
Analyze the linguistic ambiguity
The conjunction 'wa' (and) can be interpreted either as joining two separate statements or as a single expression with repetition for emphasis. This linguistic ambiguity leads to the ikhtilaf.
The same ambiguity arises in other contexts like oaths.
4
Note the absence of tarjih in this passage
The text from al-Hidayah only mentions the existence of two opinions without giving preponderance to either. This indicates that the matter is subject to ijtihad and either view may be followed.
A mufti would need to refer to other sources for tarjih.
5
Understand the practical implication
If one divorce occurs, the husband may revoke the divorce during the idda; if two, he cannot, and the marriage is dissolved after the idda. The number affects the possibility of reconciliation.
The practical difference is significant in family law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ان دخلت الدار فانت طالق واحدة وواحدۃ کے الفاظ سے طلاق دی اور عورت گھر میں داخل ہوئی ایک طلاق واقع ہوگی یا دو اقوال فقہاء
If a man says 'if you enter the house, you are divorced once and once' and the woman enters the house, will one divorce occur or two? There are two opinions of the fuqaha.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage presents a scenario where a conditional divorce is phrased with 'one and one'. When the condition (entering the house) is fulfilled, there is a difference of opinion among Hanafi jurists. Some say that 'one and one' in a conditional context results in a single divorce, treating the second 'one' as emphasis. Others hold that it results in two divorces because the condition triggers both statements. The text only mentions that there are two views without specifying which is preponderant, indicating that the matter is subject to ijtihad.

Key Terms
اقوال فقہاء (aqwāl al-fuqahā’): Opinions of the jurists; here it refers to differing Hanafi positions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the conditional 'in dakhalti al-dār fa-anti ṭāliq wāḥidatan wa wāḥidatān', how many divorces occur upon entry according to the text?
A) Always one
B) Always two
C) There are two opinions
D) None

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'wāḥidatan wa wāḥidatan' in a conditional divorce is subject to two Hanafi opinions: one or two divorces, due to linguistic ambiguity.

Kinayah Divorce: Definition and Two Types
Definition and classification of kinayah divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define kinayah divorce, distinguish its two types, and apply the Hanafi ruling on the role of intention.

Core Idea
Kinayah divorce: intention or context determines the effect, except for three words that give raj'i automatically.

In the Hanafi madhhab, divorce can be effected not only by explicit words (ṣarīḥ) but also by allusive expressions (kināyah). This lesson unpacks the definition of kinayah and its two categories as presented in al-Hidayah. Understanding this classification is crucial for correctly applying the rules of divorce in ambiguous situations.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition of kinayah
Kinayah words are those that do not directly mean divorce but can imply it through intention (niyyah) or contextual indication (dalālat al-ḥāl). Read the source passage to see that divorce occurs by these factors.
Contrast with ṣarīḥ (explicit) words that need no intention.
2
Identify the two types of kinayah
The passage states there are two types. The first type consists of three specific words that give a raj'i (revocable) divorce regardless of intention. The second type includes all other kinayah words where the effect depends on the husband's intention.
The three specific words are listed elsewhere in al-Hidayah.
3
Apply the rule for 'remaining' kinayah
For the second type, if the husband intends one divorce, one bā'in (irrevocable) divorce occurs; if he intends three, three bā'in divorces occur. If no divorce is intended, no divorce takes place.
Context can also indicate intention if the husband does not verbalize it.
4
Recognize the practical difference from other schools
The Shafi'i school holds that all kinayah words require intention and there is no fixed set giving raj'i automatically. This leads to different outcomes in similar scenarios.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf rooted in differing usul methodologies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم ثانی کنایات کا بیان الفاظ کنائی میں نیت یا دلالت حال سے طلاق واقع ہوتی ہے طلاق کنائی کی دو قسمیں تین الفاظ کنائی سے طلاق رجعی واقع ہوتی ہے تیہ کنایات سے طلاق کی نیت ہو تو ایک طلاق بائن اور اگر تین کی نیت ہو تو تین طلاقیں واقع ہوتی ہیں
Section on the second type: kinayah. In kinayah words, divorce occurs by intention or contextual indication. Kinayah divorce has two types. With three kinayah words, raj'i divorce occurs. With the remaining kinayah words, if one divorce is intended, one bā'in occurs; if three are intended, three occur.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage introduces kinayah (allusive) divorce. Unlike explicit words (ṣarīḥ), kinayah words require intention (niyyah) or circumstantial evidence (dalālat al-ḥāl) to effect divorce. The Hanafi school divides kinayah into two categories: (1) Three specific words (listed elsewhere in the text) that give a raj'i (revocable) divorce regardless of intention. (2) All other kinayah words (the 'remaining') where the number of divorces depends on the husband's intention: if he intends one, one bā'in divorce occurs; if he intends three, three occur. The key point is that with kinayah, the effect is not automatic; it follows the intention.

Key Terms
کنایہ (kināyah): Allusive word that requires intention or context to effect divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi madhhab, how many kinayah words give a raj'i divorce?
A) All
B) Three
C) None
D) It depends on intention

Key Takeaway
Kinayah divorce requires intention or context, except for three words that automatically give a revocable divorce.

Kinayah Divorce: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i Default Rulings
Hanafi ruling on kinayah and Shafi'i opposing view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi default ruling for kinayah divorce (bā'in or raj'i) and explain how the Shafi'i position differs.

Core Idea
Hanafi makes most ambiguous divorce words bā'in; Shafi'i keeps them revocable.

This lesson examines a critical disagreement between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding divorce by kinayah (allusive words). The Hanafi school, with the exception of three specific words, considers all kinayah expressions to result in a bā'in (irrevocable) divorce, regardless of the husband's intention. In contrast, Imam al-Shafi'i held that kinayah divorce is raj'i (revocable) by default. Understanding this difference is essential because the same ambiguous statement can lead to radically different marital consequences depending on which school's ruling is applied.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act in question is talaq (divorce) pronounced using kinayah (allusive) words. The Hanafi ruling is that such a divorce is bā'in (irrevocable) for the majority of kinayah words.
Three specific words form an exception; they yield raj'i divorce.
2
Understand the disagreement
The Shafi'i school holds that kinayah divorce is raj'i by default. Contrast this with the Hanafi position to grasp the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).
3
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Consider a husband who says to his wife 'You are free' (anṭi ḥurrah). Under Hanafi, this is bā'in; under Shafi'i, it is raj'i. Identify the difference in the couple's ability to reconcile.
Bā'in requires a new nikah; raj'i allows return during iddah.
4
Recognize the authority
The Hidayah relies on the Hanafi position. Know that this is the mufta bihi (operative ruling) in the Hanafi school for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مذکورہ طلاق میں بغیر نسبت طلاق ، طلاق واقع ہو جاتی ہے پہلے تین الفاظ کے علاوہ بقیہ کنایات میں طلاق بائن واقع ہوتی ہے۔ امام شافعی کا نقطہ نظر
In the mentioned divorce, without relation, divorce occurs. Apart from the first three words, in the remaining kinayah words, bā'in divorce occurs. The view of Imam al-Shafi'i.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage compares the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on kinayah divorce. In the Hanafi school, for most kinayah words (excluding the three that give raj'i), the divorce is bā'in (irrevocable) regardless of the husband's intention. However, Imam al-Shafi'i holds that kinayah divorce is always raj'i unless the husband explicitly intends bā'in or three. The Hanafi view makes kinayah more stringent: if a husband uses an allusive word, the default outcome is bā'in (unless he intended three, then it is three). The text only notes the difference without detailed rebuttal.

Key Terms
بائن (bā'in): Irrevocable divorce that severs the marriage immediately.
کنایات (kināyāt): Plural of kinayah: allusive or indirect words that can imply divorce but are not explicit terms like 'talaq'.
رجعي (rajʿī): Revocable divorce; the husband can take back his wife during the waiting period without a new marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what type of divorce results from most kinayah words?
A) Raj'i
B) Bā'in
C) No divorce
D) Three divorces

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, most kinayah (allusive) divorce words yield a bā'in (irrevocable) divorce, while the Shafi'i school treats them as raj'i (revocable).

Delegation of Divorce: Ikhtiyar (Choice)
Rulings on giving a woman choice to divorce herself

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid ikhtiyar (delegation of choice) and determine when a divorce becomes binding.

Core Idea
Ikhtiyar: wife must accept in the same session; standing up cancels the offer.

This passage explains the rules of ikhtiyar, where a husband delegates the power to divorce to his wife. The key point is that the wife's acceptance must occur in the same gathering (majlis). Understanding the conditions and variants helps avoid invalid divorces.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal nature
Ikhtiyar is a form of talaq tafwid – the husband gives the wife a choice to divorce herself. Recognize that this is a valid delegation when conditions are met.
The husband's words like 'ikhtari' or 'talliqi nafsak' initiate the process.
2
List the essential conditions
The acceptance must be in the same majlis (session). Additionally, the word 'nafs' (self) must appear in either the husband's or wife's speech. Without these, no divorce occurs.
Standing up from the majlis ends the choice.
3
Determine the effect of acceptance
If the wife accepts immediately, one bā'in (irrevocable) divorce occurs. The husband retains the right to revoke if he intended only one divorce.
If the husband says 'amruki biyadik', the divorce is revocable.
4
Analyze variants
If the husband repeats 'ikhtari' three times and the wife accepts each, three divorces occur. The phrase 'amruki biyadik fī taṭlīqihā' yields one revocable divorce.
The number of divorces depends on the wording and responses.
5
Note the consequence of leaving the majlis
Once the wife stands up, her choice expires. She cannot accept later. This prevents indefinite uncertainty.
Silence or leaving without accepting means no divorce.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter of delegation of divorce. Section on choice. Ruling on giving a woman choice by saying 'ikhtari' or 'talliqi nafsak'. The woman's choice ends when she stands from the session. If in response to 'ikhtari' she chooses herself, one bā'in divorce occurs. The word 'nafs' must be in the speech of one of the spouses. The man said 'ikhtari nafsak', the woman said 'akhtartu', then one bā'in divorce occurs. The husband said 'ikhtari', the woman said 'anā akhtāru nafsī', then divorce occurs. The husband said 'ikhtari ikhtari ikhtari' and the woman said 'akhtartu al-ūlā wa al-wusṭā wa al-ākhirah', then three divorces occur. The man said 'amruki biyadik fī taṭlīqihā' or 'ikhtari taṭlīqahā' and the woman chose herself, then one divorce occurs and the husband has the right to return.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage covers the delegation of divorce by giving the woman choice (ikhtiyār). The husband may say 'ikhtari' (choose) or 'talliqi nafsak' (divorce yourself). If she accepts during the same session (majlis), one bā'in divorce occurs. If she stands up, choice expires. The word 'nafs' (self) must appear in either the husband's or wife's speech. If the husband repeats 'ikhtari' three times and the woman responds to each, three divorces occur. If the husband says 'amruki biyadik fī taṭlīqihā' (your affair is in your hand regarding her divorce) and she chooses herself, one divorce occurs and the husband retains the right to revoke during the 'iddah.

اختیار (ikhtiyār): Delegation of choice to the wife to divorce herself.
طلاق بائن (ṭalāq bā'in): Irrevocable divorce; husband cannot take her back without a new marriage.
مجلس (majlis): Session or gathering; the period during which the offer remains open.

Delegation of Divorce: Amruki Biyadik
Rulings when husband says 'your affair is in your hand'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings when a husband says 'amruki biyadik' and determine the number of divorces based on the husband's intention and the wife's response.

Core Idea
Delegation of divorce is limited by the husband's intention and the wife's specific wording.

The phrase 'amruki biyadik' (your affair is in your hand) is a common delegation of divorce. However, its effect depends on the husband's underlying intention and the wife's exact response. This lesson clarifies the rulings for different scenarios, including time-bound delegations and session continuity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the husband's intention
Determine how many divorces the husband intended when he delegated. If he intended three, and the wife accepts the delegation, three occur. If he intended one or no specific intention, the default is one.
Intention is key; if ambiguous, the woman's response may clarify.
2
Analyse the wife's response
Classify the wife's statement: 'akhtartu nafsi biwāḥidah' (I chose myself with one) is an acceptance of the delegation, triggering the intended number. 'Tallaqtu nafsi biwāḥidah' (I divorced myself with one) is an execution of the divorce, resulting in one bā'in divorce.
The wording matters: 'akhtartu' vs 'tallaqtu' lead to different outcomes.
3
Apply the ruling
Combine intention and response: if husband intended three and wife 'akhtartu', three divorces occur. If wife says 'tallaqtu' regardless of intention, it is one bā'in.
The wife's knowledge of the husband's intention is not required; the ruling is objective.
4
Consider time restrictions
If the husband limited the delegation to a specific time or event (e.g., 'today and after tomorrow'), the option expires accordingly. The night between 'today' and 'tomorrow' is included or excluded based on phrasing.
'al-yawm wa ba‘da ghadin' excludes the night; 'al-yawm wa ghadan' includes it.
5
Evaluate session continuity
The wife's option continues as long as she remains in the same session. If she stays a full day, the option remains. Knowledge of a triggering event (e.g., arrival of a person) must be obtained for the option to be available.
If she does not know of the event until nightfall, the option is lost.

Source Text

Section on 'the affair in the hand'. If a man said 'amruki biyadik' and intended three divorces, and the woman said 'akhtartu nafsi biwāḥidatin', then three divorces occur. If the woman responded to 'amruki biyadik' with 'tallaqtu nafsi biwāḥidah' or 'akhtartu nafsi bitaṭlīqah', then one bā'in divorce occurs. If the man said 'amruki biyadik al-yawm wa ba‘da ghadin', then the night is not included. If the man said 'amruki biyadik al-yawm wa ghadan', then the night is included. If the man said 'amruki biyadik yawma yaqdamu fulān', and that person arrived but the woman did not know of his arrival until night came, then the option is no longer available to the woman. If the woman's affair was placed in her hand and she stayed in that session for a day, then the affair remains in her hand. If the woman was hearing? Then the session is considered? Or if she did not know, then which knowledge or session is considered? If the woman was standing and then sat down, she has the choice.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage details the delegation of divorce when the husband says 'amruki biyadik' (your affair is in your hand). The outcome depends on the husband's intention: if he intended three divorces, and the wife says 'I chose myself with one', three occur because her statement is interpreted as accepting the delegation which triggers the intended number. However, if she says 'I divorced myself with one' or 'I chose myself with a divorce', it is interpreted as her executing the divorce herself, resulting in one bā'in. Time restrictions: if the husband limits the authority to 'today and after tomorrow', the night between is excluded; if he says 'today and tomorrow', the night is included. If the authority is tied to a future event (like arrival of a person) and the woman does not learn of it until after nightfall, her choice expires. If she remains in the same session for a day, the authority still stands. The text also discusses the effect of witnesses and knowledge.

Key Terms
امرک بیدک (amruki biyadik): Phrase meaning 'your affair is in your hand', delegating divorce authority to the wife.
اخترت نفسی بواحدة (akhtartu nafsi biwāḥidatin): Wife's statement meaning 'I chose myself with one', interpreted as acceptance of delegation.
طلقت نفسی بتطليقة (tallaqtu nafsi bitaṭlīqah): Wife's statement meaning 'I divorced myself with a divorce', interpreted as execution of divorce.

Key Takeaway
The effect of delegating divorce depends on the husband's intention and the wife's specific wording; ignorance of these nuances leads to incorrect conclusions.

Delegation of Divorce: Talliqi Nafsak
Rulings on 'talliqi nafsak' delegation with and without numerical specification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between absolute and limited delegation of divorce (talliqi nafsak) and state the Hanafi rulings for each scenario.

Core Idea
When a husband says 'talliqi nafsak', one raj'i divorce occurs unless he specifies a number; if she exceeds his limit, the ruling is disputed.

This passage from al-Hidayah explains the rulings when a husband delegates divorce to his wife using the phrase 'talliqi nafsak' (divorce yourself). The key distinction is whether the husband specified a number (e.g., three, one) or left it absolute. The Hanafi school also addresses whether the wife's wording matters and whether the husband can revoke after delegation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the delegation phrase and any numerical specification
Check what the husband said: e.g., absolute 'talliqi nafsak' or limited to a number like 'thalāthan' or 'wāḥidatan'. If absolute, the default is one raj'i divorce if the wife says 'tallaqtu nafsi'.
If the husband uses 'mashiyyah' (whenever you wish), it gives ongoing authority.
2
Determine the wife's response
What did the wife say? Did she match the number, or exceed it? For example, if husband said 'three' and wife said 'one', she is within authorization. If husband said 'one' and wife said 'three', she exceeded.
The wife's wording matters: 'abantu nafsi' (I separate myself) or 'akhtartu nafsi' (I choose myself) may also count as divorce.
3
Apply the ruling for matching response
If the wife's response matches the husband's specification, that number of divorces occurs. If she says less, the lesser occurs. If the husband was absolute, one raj'i occurs.
The husband cannot revoke his delegation after saying 'talliqi nafsak'.
4
Apply the ruling for exceeding response
If the husband specifies a number and the wife exceeds it, there is a difference of opinion (ikhtilaf) among Hanafi jurists: some say no divorce occurs, others say one occurs. The text presents both views.
This ikhtilaf only arises when the delegation is limited to a number.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المشية عورت کو طلقی نفسک کہا کوئی نیت نہیں تھی یا ایک کی نیت کی تھی عورت نے طلقت نفسی کہا تو ایک طلاق رجعی واقع ہو جائے گی شوہر نے کہا طلقی نفسک عورت نے کہا ابنت نفسی یا اخترت نفسی کہا تو کون سے لفظ سے طلاق واقع ہوگی طلقی نفسک کہہ کر شو ہر قول سے رجوع نہیں کر سکتا طلقی نفسک متی شنت کہا تو عورت مجلس اور مجلس کے بعد اپنے آپ کو طلاق دے سکتی ہے طلقی نفسک ثلاثاً کہا عورت نے طلقت واحدۃ کہا تو ایک طلاق واقع ہوگی مرد نے طلقی نفسک واحدة کہا فطلقت نفسها ثلاثا تو کوئی طلاق واقع ہوگی یا نہیں ، اقوال فقہاء
Section on will (mashiyyah). If a woman is told 'talliqi nafsak' and there was no intention or intention of one, and the woman said 'tallaqtu nafsi', then one raj'i divorce occurs. If the husband said 'talliqi nafsak' and the woman said 'abantu nafsi' or 'akhtartu nafsi', then by which words does divorce occur? After saying 'talliqi nafsak', the husband cannot revoke his words. If he said 'talliqi nafsak matā shi'ti', then the woman can divorce herself in the session and after. If he said 'talliqi nafsak thalāthan' and the woman said 'tallaqtu wāḥidatan', then one divorce occurs. If the man said 'talliqi nafsak wāḥidatan' and she divorced herself three times, will any divorce occur or not? Opinions of the fuqaha.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage covers the 'mashiyyah' (will) form of delegation, where the husband says 'talliqi nafsak' (divorce yourself). If he had no specific intention or intended one, and she says 'tallaqtu nafsi' (I divorced myself), one raj'i divorce occurs. The husband cannot revoke his authorization once given. If the phrase is absolute ('whenever you wish'), she can divorce immediately or later. If he says 'three' and she only says 'one', only one occurs. If he says 'one' and she says 'three', there is a difference of opinion: does any divorce occur? Some Hanafi jurists say none, because she exceeded the authorization; others say one occurs. The text presents this as ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
مشیت (mashiyyah): Will or wish; here it refers to delegation phrased as 'when you wish'.
طلقی نفسک (talliqi nafsak): Divorce yourself; phrase of delegation of divorce.

Requirement of the Word 'Nafs' in Delegation of Divorce
Ruling on requirement of 'nafs' in tafwid al-talaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition requiring the word 'nafs' in tafwid al-talaq and apply it to different wordings to determine the resulting divorce.

Core Idea
The word 'nafs' is a necessary condition in tafwid al-talaq to specify the wife's self as the subject of choice.

Delegation of divorce (tafwid al-talaq) is a subtle area where precise wording determines legal effects. The Hanafi school stipulates a specific condition: the word 'nafs' must appear in the speech of either spouse. This lesson examines the exact wordings that yield valid delegation and their consequences. You will learn to identify whether a given formula meets the condition and what type of divorce results.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initiating wording
Look at what the husband said. Does it contain the word 'nafs'? For example, 'Ikhtari nafsik' contains it, but 'Ikhtari' alone does not.
2
Identify the wife's response
Check if the wife's response includes 'nafs'. For instance, 'Ana akhtar nafsi' contains it, while 'Ikhtartu' does not.
3
Check the condition
Determine if 'nafs' appears in at least one of the two statements. If it does, the delegation is valid; if not, it is invalid.
The condition is met if either spouse says 'nafs'.
4
Determine the number of divorces
If the husband repeats the delegation phrase, match the wife's response to each instance. If she chooses separately each time, multiple divorces occur.
In the example with three 'Ikhtari', three divorces occur because she chooses each separately.
5
Classify the type of divorce
Identify whether the divorce is bain (irrevocable) or raj'i (revocable). The text states that in one scenario, the divorce is bain; in another, the husband retains the right to return.
The type depends on the specific wording used by the husband.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لفظ " نفس" زوجین میں سے کسی ایک کے کلام میں ہونا ضروری ہے
مرد نے کہا اختاری نفسک عورت نے کہا اخترت تو ایک طلاق بائنہ واقع ہو جائے گی شوہر نے اختاری کہا عورت انا اختار نفسی کہا تو طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی
شوہر نے اختاری اختاری اختاری کہا اور عورت نے اخترت الاولیٰ والوسطى والاخيرة کہا تو تین طلاقیں واقع ہو جائیں گی
مرد نے امرک بیدک فی تطلیقہ یا اختاری تطلیقہ کہا اور عورت نے اپنے نفس کو اختیار کر لیا تو ایک طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی اور شوہر رجوع کا مالک ہوگا
The word 'nafs' is necessary in the speech of one of the spouses. [If] the man said 'Ikhtari nafsik' (choose yourself) and the woman said 'Ashtar' (I chose), then one irrevocable divorce (talaq bain) occurs. [If] the husband said 'Ikhtari' and the woman said 'Ana akhtar nafsi' (I choose myself), then divorce occurs. [If] the husband said 'Ikhtari, ikhtari, ikhtari' and the woman said 'Ikhtartu al-ula, al-wusta, al-akhira' (I chose the first, middle, last), then three divorces occur. [If] the man said 'Amruki bi-yadiki fi talaqiha' or 'Ikhtari talaqiha' (your affair is in your hand regarding her divorce or choose her divorce), and the woman chose herself, then one divorce occurs and the husband has the right to take her back.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the section on delegation of divorce (tafwid al-talaq). The key condition identified is that the word 'nafs' (self) must appear in the utterance of either the husband or the wife when granting her the choice to divorce herself. If the husband says 'Ikhtari nafsik' (choose yourself) and the woman responds with 'Ikhtartu' (I chose), one irrevocable divorce (talaq bain) takes place. If the husband merely says 'Ikhtari' (choose) without the word 'nafs', and the woman says 'Ana akhtar nafsi' (I choose myself), divorce also occurs because her speech contains 'nafs'. The example of repeating 'Ikhtari' three times with her choosing each separately results in three divorces. In the case where the husband delegates by saying 'Amruki bi-yadiki fi talaqiha' (your affair is in your hand regarding her divorce) or 'Ikhtari talaqiha' (choose her divorce), and the woman chooses herself, one revocable divorce occurs, and the husband retains the right of return (ruju'). These rulings establish the precise verbal conditions for valid tafwid.

Key Terms
نفس (nafs): The word 'self' used in the context of divorce delegation; its presence in the wording is a condition for validity.
اختار (ikhtiyar): Choosing or having the option to divorce oneself upon delegation.
طلاق بائنہ (talaq bain): Irrevocable divorce that severs the marital bond immediately.
طلاق رجعی (talaq raj'i): Revocable divorce that allows the husband to take back his wife during the waiting period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The word 'nafs' must appear in the speech of one spouse.
C) The woman must be alone in the house.
D) The husband must intend a specific number of divorces.

Key Takeaway
For a valid delegation of divorce, the word 'nafs' must be present in the speech of at least one spouse.

8 min
Basic Delegation: 'Tallaqi Nafsik' and the Wife's Response
Basic ruling on unrestricted delegation of divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a husband delegates divorce to his wife without specifying a number, and identify the effect of the wife's alternative wording (e.g., 'Abantu nafsi' or 'Ikhtartu nafsi').

Core Idea
A husband's delegation with ‘divorce yourself’ triggers one revocable divorce upon the wife's pronouncement, and he cannot retract.

This lesson focuses on the basic Hanafi ruling of tafwid al-talaq (delegation of divorce). When a husband tells his wife 'Tallaqi nafsik' (divorce yourself) without specifying a number or making it conditional, the wife has the authority to divorce herself. If she does so, one revocable divorce occurs. The lesson also covers the effect of the wife using synonymous phrases like 'Abantu nafsi' (I separated myself) or 'Ikhtartu nafsi' (I chose myself).

Guided Path
1
Identify the delegation phrase
The husband says 'Tallaqi nafsik' (divorce yourself). Note that he does not specify a number or condition.
2
Determine the wife's response
The wife pronounces divorce using words like 'Tallaqt nafsi' (I divorced myself), 'Abantu nafsi' (I separated myself), or 'Ikhtartu nafsi' (I chose myself).
These are valid expressions of divorce.
3
Apply the ruling
If the wife pronounces divorce, one revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) occurs immediately. The husband cannot retract his delegation after the wife acts.
If the wife does not act, no divorce occurs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المشية
عورت کو طلقی نفسک کہا کوئی نیت نہیں تھی یا ایک کی نیت کی تھی عورت نے طلقت نفسی کہا تو ایک طلاق رجعی واقع ہو جائے گی
شوہر نے کہا طلقی نفسک عورت نے کہا ابنت نفسی یا اخترت نفسی کہا تو کون سے لفظ سے طلاق واقع ہوگی طلقی نفسک کہہ کر شو ہر قول سے رجوع نہیں کر سکتا
طلقی نفسک متی شنت کہا تو عورت مجلس اور مجلس کے بعد اپنے آپ کو طلاق دے سکتی ہے
طلقی نفسک ثلاثاً کہا عورت نے طلقت واحدۃ کہا تو ایک طلاق واقع ہوگی مرد نے طلقی نفسک واحدة کہا فطلقت نفسها ثلاثا تو کوئی طلاق واقع ہوگی یا نہیں ، اقوال فقہاء
شوہر نے طلاق رجعی دینے کا کہا اس نے بائنہ دی یا بائنہ دینے کا کہا اس نے رجعی طلاق دی مرد نے جس طلاق کا حکم کیا وہی واقع ہوگی
مرد نے طلقی نفسک ثلثا ان شئت کہا عورت نے ایک طلاق دے دی تو کوئی طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی طلقی نفسک واحدۃ ان شئتِ کہا عورت نے تین واقع کیں تو کوئی طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی مرد نے انت طالق ان شئت کہا عورت نے شنت ان شنت کہا مرد نے شنت کہا تو امر بالید باطل ہو جائے گا اور اگر مرد نے انت طالق اذا شئت او اذا ما شئت او فى شئت او في ما شئت کہا عورت نے اس امر کور د کر دیا تو یہ رد ہوگا یا نہیں
مرد نے انت طالق كلما شنت کہا تو عورت اپنے آپ کو کتنی طلاقیں دے سکتی ہے مرد نے انت طالق حيث شنت او این شئت کہا طلاق نہیں ہوگی۔ یہاں تک کہ عورت چاہے اگر مجلس میں کھڑی ہوگئی عورت کیلئے مشیت نہیں ہے مرد نے انت طالق کیف شنت کہا تو طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی اور شوہر رجوع کا مالک ہوگا مرد نے انت طالق کم شنت او ما شئت کہا تو عورت اپنے آپ کو کتنی طلاقیں دے سکتی ہے مرد نے طلقی نفسک من ثلاث ما شئت کہا تو عورت اپنے آپ کو کتنی طلاقیں دے سکتی ہے
Section on conditional will. He told the woman 'Tallaqi nafsik' (divorce yourself) with no intention or intention of one, and she said 'Tallaqt nafsi' (I divorced myself) - then one revocable divorce occurs. The husband said 'Tallaqi nafsik', the woman said 'Abantu nafsi' (I separated myself) or 'Ikhtartu nafsi' (I chose myself) - then from which word does divorce occur? The husband cannot retract from his saying 'Tallaqi nafsik'. If he says 'Tallaqi nafsik mati shi't' (divorce yourself whenever you wish), then the woman can divorce herself during the assembly and after the assembly. If he says 'Tallaqi nafsik thalathan' and she said 'Tallaqt wahidah' - then one divorce occurs. If the man said 'Tallaqi nafsik wahidah' and she divorced herself three times, does any divorce occur or not? [There are] sayings of the jurists. The husband said he gave revocable divorce, she gave irrevocable; or he said irrevocable, she gave revocable; the divorce that the husband commanded is what will occur. The man said 'Tallaqi nafsik thalithan in shi't' (divorce yourself three if you wish) - she gave one divorce, then no divorce occurs. If he said 'Tallaqi nafsik wahidah in shi't' and she gave three, then no divorce occurs. The man said 'Anti taliq in shi't' (you are divorced if you wish), the woman said 'Sha'tu in sha'tu' (I wished if I wished), the man said 'Sha'tu' (I wished) - then the amr bi yad becomes void. And if the man said 'Anti taliq idha shi'tu' (you are divorced when I wish) or 'idha ma shi'tu' or 'fi shi'tu' or 'fi ma shi'tu', and the woman refused that matter - does this count as rejection or not? The man said 'Anti taliq kullama shi'ti' (you are divorced whenever you wish) - how many divorces can the woman give herself? The man said 'Anti taliq haythu shi'ti' or 'ayna shi'ti' (you are divorced wherever you wish) - no divorce occurs until the woman wishes? If she stood up in the assembly, there is no option for her. The man said 'Anti taliq kayfa shi'ti' (you are divorced however you wish) - then divorce occurs and the husband has the right to take her back. The man said 'Anti taliq kama shi'ti' or 'ma shi'ti' (you are divorced as you wish) - how many divorces can the woman give herself? The man said 'Tallaqi nafsik min thalath ma shi'ti' (divorce yourself from three as you wish) - how many divorces can she give herself?
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage is from the section 'Fasl fi al-Mashi'ah' (Chapter on Conditional Will) in Hanafi divorce law. It covers cases where the husband ties the divorce to the wife's will (mashi'ah) or wish. The opening ruling states that if the husband says 'Tallaqi nafsik' (divorce yourself) without specifying number, and the wife divorces herself, one revocable divorce occurs. If the wife uses different wording like 'Abantu nafsi' (I separated myself) or 'Ikhtartu nafsi' (I chose myself), divorce still occurs, but the husband cannot retract his permission. The option may be time-unlimited if the phrase 'mati shi't' (whenever you wish) is used. Discrepancies between the number ordered and number given are resolved: if he orders three and she gives one, only one falls. If he orders one and she gives three, does any fall? The jurists have different views (probably difference between Abu Hanifa and others). If the husband specifies revocable or irrevocable, the wife must follow his specification. Conditional phrases like 'in shi't' (if you wish) restrict the divorce to that condition: if she exceeds, no divorce falls. Various prepositions like 'kullama shi'ti', 'haythu shi'ti', 'kayfa shi'ti' are discussed with their implications. For 'kullama shi'ti' (whenever you wish), she can give herself multiple divorces repeatedly. For 'haythu shi'ti' (wherever), no divorce until she actually wishes in that place. For 'kayfa shi'ti' (however), one divorce occurs and is revocable. The exact number for 'kama shi'ti' (as you wish) is also discussed; the text suggests she can give as many as she wishes. Finally, 'min thalath ma shi'ti' gives her the right to choose any number up to three.

Key Terms
مشية (mashi'ah): Will or wish; in this context, the condition in divorce delegation that ties the divorce to the woman's will.
ان شئت (in shi'ta): If you wish; a conditional phrase restricting the divorce to the woman's wish.
كلما شئت (kullama shi'ta): Whenever you wish; allows repeated divorces at different times.
طلقی نفسک (tallaqi nafsik): Divorce yourself; the phrase used by the husband to delegate the power of divorce to his wife.
أبنت نفسی (abantu nafsi): I separated myself; a phrase used by the wife to effect divorce, considered valid.
اخترت نفسی (ikhtartu nafsi): I chose myself; another phrase indicating divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says 'Tallaqi nafsik' without specifying a number. The wife says 'Abantu nafsi'. What occurs?
A) No divorce occurs because she did not say 'Tallaqt'.
B) One revocable divorce occurs.
C) One irrevocable divorce occurs.
D) The husband can cancel the delegation.

Key Takeaway
Unrestricted delegation ‘Tallaqi nafsik’ gives the wife authority to effect one revocable divorce by any clear expression.

Talaq — Divorce

Time and Number Specifications in Delegation
Rulings on time and number specification in divorce delegation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the outcome when the husband specifies a time (e.g., 'mati shi't') or a number (e.g., 'thalathan') in the delegation of divorce, and when the wife's action contradicts the specified number.

Core Idea
Specifying ‘whenever you wish’ gives unlimited time; specifying a number binds the wife: she cannot exceed or fall short without consequence.

This lesson covers two types of specifications in the delegation of divorce: timing and number. If the husband says 'Tallaqi nafsik mati shi't' (divorce yourself whenever you wish), the wife can act at any time, even after the assembly. If he specifies a number, such as 'thalathan' (three), the wife must divorce exactly that number. The passage also addresses cases where the wife divorces fewer or more than specified, and the difference of opinion among the jurists (aqwal fuqaha) on whether any divorce occurs if she exceeds.

Guided Path
1
Identify if a time condition is attached
Look for phrases like 'mati shi't' (whenever you wish) which indicate the wife can act anytime.
If no time condition, the wife can act only in the same assembly? Actually, the text says she can act in and after the assembly.
2
Identify if a number is specified
Look for explicit numbers like 'thalathan' (three) or 'wahidah' (one).
3
Determine if the wife's action matches the specification
Compare the number the wife pronounces with the number the husband authorized.
4
Apply the ruling for matching or mismatching numbers
If she matches, that number occurs. If she does fewer, only what she gave occurs. If she exceeds, the text raises the question: does any divorce occur? The jurists differ.
The text mentions 'aqwal fuqaha' without resolving which is mufta bihi.

Conditional Delegation with 'In Shi't' (If You Wish)
Rulings on conditional delegation with 'in shi't'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling when the husband conditions the delegation on the wife's wish (e.g., 'in shi't'), and determine the outcome when the wife's action exceeds or falls short of the condition.

Core Idea
When divorce is conditioned on the wife's wish, she must act exactly as specified; any deviation voids the divorce.

This lesson addresses the conditional delegation where the husband says 'in shi't' (if you wish) after specifying a number. For example, 'Tallaqi nafsik thalithan in shi't' (divorce yourself three if you wish). In this case, the wife's authority is strictly conditional on her wish and the specified number. The text provides two scenarios: if he says three and she gives one, no divorce occurs; if he says one and she gives three, no divorce occurs. The lesson also covers a more complex case where the husband says 'Anti taliq in shi't' and the wife responds with a conditional statement, leading to the annulment of the delegation (amr bi yad becomes void).

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional phrase
Look for 'in shi't' or 'in shi'ti' after the delegation phrase.
2
Note the specified number and condition
The husband specifies a number (e.g., thalithan, wahidah) and attaches the condition that the wife must wish it.
3
Compare the wife's action with the specified number
The wife's action must match exactly the number and the condition. If she does a different number, she did not fulfill the condition.
4
Apply the ruling: no divorce if mismatch
The text explicitly says that if he says three and she gives one, no divorce occurs. Similarly, if he says one and she gives three, no divorce occurs.
This contrasts with the unconditional case where giving fewer is effective.

Source Text

The man said 'Tallaqi nafsik thalithan in shi't' (divorce yourself three if you wish) - she gave one divorce, then no divorce occurs. If he said 'Tallaqi nafsik wahidah in shi't' and she gave three, then no divorce occurs. The man said 'Anti taliq in shi't' (you are divorced if you wish), the woman said 'Sha'tu in sha'tu' (I wished if I wished), the man said 'Sha'tu' (I wished) - then the amr bi yad becomes void. And if the man said 'Anti taliq idha shi'tu' (you are divorced when I wish) or 'idha ma shi'tu' or 'fi shi'tu' or 'fi ma shi'tu', and the woman refused that matter - does this count as rejection or not? The man said 'Anti taliq kullama shi'ti' (you are divorced whenever you wish) - how many divorces can the woman give herself? The man said 'Anti taliq haythu shi'ti' or 'ayna shi'ti' (you are divorced wherever you wish) - no divorce occurs until the woman wishes? If she stood up in the assembly, there is no option for her. The man said 'Anti taliq kayfa shi'ti' (you are divorced however you wish) - then divorce occurs and the husband has the right to take her back. The man said 'Anti taliq kama shi'ti' or 'ma shi'ti' (you are divorced as you wish) - how many divorces can the woman give herself? The man said 'Tallaqi nafsik min thalath ma shi'ti' (divorce yourself from three as you wish) - how many divorces can she give herself?
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage is from the section 'Fasl fi al-Mashi'ah' (Chapter on Conditional Will) in Hanafi divorce law. It covers cases where the husband ties the divorce to the wife's will (mashi'ah) or wish. The opening ruling states that if the husband says 'Tallaqi nafsik' (divorce yourself) without specifying number, and the wife divorces herself, one revocable divorce occurs. If the wife uses different wording like 'Abantu nafsi' (I separated myself) or 'Ikhtartu nafsi' (I chose myself), divorce still occurs, but the husband cannot retract his permission. The option may be time-unlimited if the phrase 'mati shi't' (whenever you wish) is used. Discrepancies between the number ordered and number given are resolved: if he orders three and she gives one, only one falls. If he orders one and she gives three, does any fall? The jurists have different views (probably difference between Abu Hanifa and others). If the husband specifies revocable or irrevocable, the wife must follow his specification. Conditional phrases like 'in shi't' (if you wish) restrict the divorce to that condition: if she exceeds, no divorce falls. Various prepositions like 'kullama shi'ti', 'haythu shi'ti', 'kayfa shi'ti' are discussed with their implications. For 'kullama shi'ti' (whenever you wish), she can give herself multiple divorces repeatedly. For 'haythu shi'ti' (wherever), no divorce until she actually wishes in that place. For 'kayfa shi'ti' (however), one divorce occurs and is revocable. The exact number for 'kama shi'ti' (as you wish) is also discussed; the text suggests she can give as many as she wishes. Finally, 'min thalath ma shi'ti' gives her the right to choose any number up to three.

Key Terms
ان شئت (in shi'ta): If you wish; a conditional phrase making the delegation subject to the wife's wish and strict compliance.
امر بالید (amr bi yad): The matter in hand; refers to the delegation of divorce. Becoming void means the delegation is cancelled.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Husband says 'Tallaqi nafsik thalithan in shi'ti' (divorce yourself three if you wish). Wife says 'Tallaqt nafsi wahidah' (one). What is the outcome?
A) One divorce occurs.
B) Three divorces occur.
C) No divorce occurs.
D) The delegation becomes void.

Key Takeaway
Conditional delegation with ‘in shi’t’ requires exact compliance; any deviation results in no divorce.

Various Prepositions in Delegation: Kullama, Haythu, Kayfa, Kama, Min Thalath
Rulings on various prepositions in divorce delegation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi rulings for different prepositional phrases (kullama, haythu, kayfa, kama, min thalath) used in delegating divorce, and determine the number of divorces the wife can give.

Core Idea
Prepositions like ‘whenever’, ‘wherever’, ‘however’ determine the scope of the wife's authority in delegated divorce.

This lesson covers the final part of the passage, which deals with various prepositions used in delegation phrases: 'kullama shi'ti' (whenever you wish), 'haythu shi'ti' or 'ayna shi'ti' (wherever you wish), 'kayfa shi'ti' (however you wish), 'kama shi'ti' or 'ma shi'ti' (as you wish), and 'min thalath ma shi'ti' (from three as you wish). Each phrase carries a different legal effect regarding how many divorces the wife can give and whether the husband retains the right to take her back.

Guided Path
1
Identify the preposition used
Determine which word (kullama, haythu, kayfa, kama, min thalath) is used.
2
Apply the corresponding ruling
Each preposition has a specific ruling: kullama allows repeated divorces; haythu/ayna require actual wish at that place; kayfa gives one revocable divorce; kama/ma gives unlimited divorces; min thalath gives up to three.
Refer to the commentary for details.
3
Note the husband's right of return
For 'kayfa shi'ti', the text says the husband has the right to take her back (raj'a). For others, it may be revocable or irrevocable.
4
Determine the number of divorces possible
Based on the preposition, calculate the maximum number of divorces the wife can give.
For kullama and kama/ma, it seems unlimited. For min thalath, up to three.

Understanding Conditional Divorce Oaths (Ta'liq al-Talaq)
General rules and specific cases of conditional divorce oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid conditional divorce oath and apply them to scenarios involving pre-marriage and post-marriage conditions.

Core Idea
A conditional divorce oath is only valid if the woman is your wife at the time of the oath.

Divorce can be made dependent on a condition. The Hanafi school provides clear rules about when such conditions take effect. This lesson explains the basic principles and exceptions based on the classical text from Bāb al-Aymān fī al-Ṭalāq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of suspension
The text distinguishes between suspending divorce on marriage itself and on a condition. Note the difference: the first triggers at marriage, the second when the condition is fulfilled.
2
Apply the general rule for pre-marriage oaths
If a man says to a non-mahram woman 'If you enter the house, you are divorced' and later marries her, the divorce does not occur even if she enters. Recognize this as a key exception.
The woman must be his wife at the time of the oath.
3
Understand the effect of 'kullama' (whenever)
The phrase 'kullama' indicates repetition. The text mentions that cancelation of ownership (e.g., divorce) does not invalidate the oath, so the condition remains active for future events.
The oath 'Whenever I marry a woman, she is divorced' applies to each subsequent marriage.
4
Analyze disputes over conditions
When the condition is known only to the wife (e.g., her internal state), her word is accepted concerning her own right (talaq) but not for others (e.g., slave's freedom).
This follows the principle that a person's statement is presumed truthful about matters within their own knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الايمان في الطلاق
طلاق کو نکاح پر معلق کیا تو نکاح کے بعد طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی اگر طلاق کو شرط پر معلق کیا تو شرط کے بعد طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی
قاعده کلیه
اجنبیہ کو کہا ان دخلت الدار فانت طالق پھر اس سے نکاح کر لیا وہ گھر میں داخل ہوئی طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی
الفاظ شرط
جب شرط پائی جائے تو یمین محل ہو کر ختم ہو جاتی ہے
كلما دخلت الدار فانت طالق پر متفرع مسئله
كلما تزوجت امرأة فهي طالق سے معلق کرنے کا حکم یمین کے بعد ملک کا زوال اس کو باطل نہیں کرتا
اگر شرط میں زوجین کا اختلاف ہو جائے تو کس کا قول معتبر ہے
اگر شرط ایسی ہے کہ عورت ہی سے معلوم ہو سکتی ہے تو عورت کا قول اس کے حق میں معتبر ہوگا
شوہر نے کہا ان كنت تحبين ان يعذبک الله في نار جهنم فانت طالق و عبدی حر عورت نے کہا احبہ تو یہ قول عورت کے حق میں معتبر ہے اور غلام کے حق میں نہیں اسی طرح شوہر نے کہا ان كنت تحبيني فان طالق وهذه معك
Chapter on oaths in divorce. If divorce is suspended on marriage, then divorce occurs after marriage. If divorce is suspended on a condition, then divorce occurs after the condition. General rule: He said to a strange woman (non-mahram) 'If you enter the house, you are divorced', then he married her, and she entered the house - divorce does not occur. Words of condition: When the condition is fulfilled, the oath is fulfilled and thus ends. A subordinate issue on 'kullama dakhalta al-dār...' (whenever you enter the house...). The ruling of suspending with 'kullama tazawwajtu imra'atan fahiya taliq' (whenever I marry a woman, she is divorced) - the cancellation of ownership after the oath does not invalidate it. If the spouses differ regarding the condition, whose statement is accepted? If the condition is such that only the wife can know, then her word is accepted concerning her own right. The husband said 'If you love that Allah punish you in the fire of Hell, then you are divorced and my slave is free'. The wife said 'I love it' - this statement is accepted concerning the wife's right but not concerning the slave. Similarly, the husband said 'If you love me, then you are divorced and this (slave) is with you?'
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage begins the chapter on oaths in divorce (Báb al-Aymān fī al-Ṭalāq) in the Hanafi fiqh. It states a foundational rule: if divorce is suspended on marriage (i.e., 'if I marry you, you are divorced'), it takes effect after marriage. If suspended on a condition (like entering a house), it takes effect after the condition is met. A general rule (qa'ida kulliya): if a man says to a non-mahram woman 'If you enter the house, you are divorced', then afterwards marries her, and she enters the house, divorce does not occur because the oath was made before marriage and is void now? Actually, Hanafis rule that such an oath is not binding because at the time of oath, she was not his wife, so the condition cannot be fulfilled after marriage. The discussion then moves to conditional words: 'kullama' (whenever) implies repetition. A sub-issue: if a man says 'Whenever I marry a woman, she is divorced', and then he marries a woman, the divorce falls. The cancellation of ownership (i.e., if he divorces her and then remarries) does not invalidate the oath; the same woman again becomes subject to the oath? But the text says the oath is fulfilled and ends when condition is met. The final part addresses disputes over conditions: if the condition is known only to the wife (like her love for punishment), her word is believed regarding her own talaq but not regarding the slave's freedom. This is based on the principle that a person's word is accepted about matters within their own knowledge.

Key Terms
یمین (yamin): Oath; here refers to a conditional divorce statement that functions like an oath.
تعلیق (ta'liq): Suspending divorce upon a condition.
شرط (shart): Condition that triggers divorce when fulfilled.
كلما (kullama): Whenever; indicates repetition, so the condition applies each time the event occurs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Only one divorce occurs.
D) It depends on his intention.

Key Takeaway
A conditional divorce oath is only valid if the woman addressed is your wife at the moment the oath is made.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
General Rules for Conditional Divorce and the Ajnabiyyah Case
States general rule and a specific case of conditional divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two general rules of conditional divorce and apply them to distinguish when a conditional divorce takes effect or not, specifically in the case of a statement made to a non-wife.

Core Idea
A conditional divorce only applies if the woman was your wife at the moment you spoke.

In this lesson, we examine a passage from the chapter on oaths in divorce that establishes the foundational rules for conditional divorce (ta'liq al-talaq). You will learn two key principles: how divorce takes effect if suspended upon marriage versus upon a condition, and why a conditional statement made to a non-wife (ajnabiyyah) has no effect even after marriage. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion about the validity of divorce in pre-marriage scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two general rules
The passage first gives two rules: (1) If divorce is made conditional upon marriage itself (e.g., 'when I marry you, you are divorced'), the divorce occurs immediately after the marriage contract. (2) If divorce is made conditional upon a specific future event (e.g., 'if you enter the house'), the divorce occurs only when that event happens.
These rules assume that the statement was made while the woman was already the man's wife, except in the first case where the condition is the marriage itself.
2
State the general principle (qāʿida kulliyya)
The implicit general principle is that a conditional divorce is valid only if the person was the man's wife at the time the condition was stated. The legal capacity (mahall) for divorce must exist at the utterance, not later.
This principle is illustrated by the ajnabiyyah case.
3
Analyze the ajnabiyyah case
The man says to a non-wife (ajnabiyyah) 'If you enter the house, you are divorced.' He later marries her, and she enters the house. According to the Hanafi school, no divorce occurs. The reason: she was not his wife when the statement was made, so the statement has no legal effect.
The fulfillment of the condition after marriage does not retroactively apply the divorce.
4
Identify the underlying legal reasoning (taʿlīl)
The exercise of divorce (talaq) is only valid within an existing marriage at the time of the pronouncement. A pre-marriage statement cannot create a future right to divorce because the marriage did not exist at that time. Thus, the condition, even if fulfilled after marriage, is irrelevant.
This reasoning is specific to the Hanafi school and leads to a different conclusion than some other schools.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Some schools (e.g., Shafiʿi) consider the man's intent: if he intended her to be his future wife, the conditional divorce might take effect. The Hanafi school strictly adheres to the time of utterance, disregarding future intentions. This ikhtilaf has practical consequences in cases of pre-marriage vows.
The Hanafi position is muftā bihi and is based on Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الايمان في الطلاق
طلاق کو نکاح پر معلق کیا تو نکاح کے بعد طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی اگر طلاق کو شرط پر معلق کیا تو شرط کے بعد طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی
قاعده کلیه
اجنبیہ کو کہا ان دخلت الدار فانت طالق پھر اس سے نکاح کر لیا وہ گھر میں داخل ہوئی طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی
Chapter of Oaths in Divorce. If divorce is suspended upon marriage, then divorce will occur after marriage. If divorce is suspended upon a condition, then divorce will occur after the condition. General principle. If a man said to a non-marriageable woman: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced,' then he married her, and she entered the house, divorce will not occur.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on oaths in divorce (باب الايمان في الطلاق) establishes two fundamental principles of conditional divorce in the Hanafi school. First, if a divorce is made conditional upon marriage (i.e., 'when I marry you, you are divorced'), the divorce takes effect immediately after the marriage contract, regardless of any subsequent acts. Second, if the divorce is conditional upon a specific condition (e.g., entering a house), the divorce only takes effect when that condition is fulfilled. The general principle (قاعده کلیه) is then illustrated: if a man says to a non-wife (اجنبیہ) 'if you enter the house, you are divorced,' and later marries her, her subsequent entry into the house does not trigger the divorce. This is because the initial statement was directed at a person who was not his wife at the time, and the condition (entry) was not suspended on the marriage itself but on a separate future act. The divorce is not valid because the person was not subject to his right of divorce at the time of the utterance.

Key Terms
تعلیق (ta'liq): Suspending the occurrence of divorce upon the occurrence of a future condition or event.
اجنبیہ (ajnabiyyah): A woman who is not one's wife (i.e., a non-marriageable or stranger woman).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) The marriage is invalid.

Key Takeaway
A conditional divorce only takes effect if the person was one's wife at the time of the conditional statement, regardless of later marriage.

Divorce via Kinayah: The Wife's Utterance of 'احسك'
Ruling on the effect of wife saying 'I am disgusted'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the wife's utterance of 'احسك' and identify it as a kinayah expression that triggers talaq but not ʿitq.

Core Idea
Not every word of disgust counts; only recognized kinayah triggers divorce, and it does not free a slave.

In Hanafi fiqh, divorce can be effected through explicit (sarih) or implicit (kinayah) expressions. This passage presents a specific kinayah: the wife saying 'احسك'. The ruling clarifies that such an utterance leads to divorce but does not free a slave, even if the husband had linked freedom to such a statement. Understanding these nuances prevents misapplication in matters of family law and manumission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the wife uttering 'احسك'. The ruling is that it causes divorce (talaq) but does not free a slave.
This is a categorical statement without conditions.
2
Recognize 'احسك' as kinayah
This word is a kinayah (implicit expression) for divorce. In Hanafi fiqh, kinayah words only effect divorce if accompanied by intention (niyyah). The passage does not mention intention, but it is assumed.
Other kinayah examples include 'اذهبي' (go away) or 'انفصلي' (separate).
3
Distinguish between talaq and ʿitq
While the utterance results in divorce, it does not free a slave. This implies that the word 'احسك' is not considered a valid expression for manumission (itq).
The ruling may be based on the principle that a single utterance cannot effect two different legal consequences unless explicitly intended.
4
Memorize the ruling for application
This is a concise ruling to be memorized. In practice, a Hanafi mufti would apply this rule when a similar case arises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عورت نے احسك کہا تو طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی اور غلام آزاد نہ ہوگا
If a woman says 'I am disgusted', divorce will occur, and the slave will not be freed.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This is a ruling on conditional utterances in divorce. The passage states that if the wife utters the word 'احسك' (interpreted as a statement of disgust or hatred), it results in the occurrence of divorce. However, it does not free a slave (if the husband had conditioned slave freedom on such an utterance). This is a straightforward ruling from the Hanafi school, focusing on the legal effect of specific words.

Key Terms
احسك (ahsak): An Arabic/Urdu term meaning 'I am disgusted' or 'I am fed up', used as a kinayah (implicit) expression for divorce.

Conditional Divorce on Menstruation: The Three-Day Blood Requirement
Ruling on condition of menstruation requiring three days of continuous blood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition that a conditional divorce based on menstruation requires three continuous days of blood flow.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, mere spotting does not trigger a conditional divorce based on menstruation; blood must flow continuously for three days.

This lesson examines a specific ruling in Hanafi family law: when a husband conditions divorce on his wife's menstruation, the mere sight of blood does not suffice. The Hanafi school defines menstruation (hayd) as blood that lasts a minimum of three full days. Understanding this condition is crucial for correctly applying such conditional divorces.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The husband's conditional statement 'When you menstruate, you are divorced' is a suspended divorce (talaq mu'allaq). The ruling is that divorce does not occur until the condition is met.
2
Identify the condition
The condition is 'menstruation' (hayd). The key is what constitutes hayd in Hanafi fiqh.
3
State the required duration
Hanafi school requires blood to flow continuously for three days (minimum hayd). Shorter flow is not hayd but istihadah (non-menstrual bleeding).
The three-day minimum is unique to the Hanafi school; other schools have different minima.
4
Apply to the scenario
The woman sees blood. This does not automatically trigger divorce. Divorce is suspended until three days of continuous flow are confirmed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شوہر نے کہا اذا حضت فانت طالق عورت نے خون دیکھا طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی یہاں تک کہ تین دن تک خون جاری رہے
The husband said: 'When you menstruate, you are divorced.' The woman saw blood; divorce will not occur until blood continues for three days.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This ruling explains that if a husband conditions divorce on menstruation (hayd), the mere sighting of blood does not trigger divorce. The blood must flow continuously for three full days (72 hours) to constitute a valid menstrual period. This is based on the Hanafi definition of hayd as blood that lasts at least three days.

Key Terms
حیض (hayd): Menstrual blood in Hanafi fiqh, defined as blood that flows from the uterus and lasts a minimum of three days and a maximum of ten.
طلاق معلق (talaq mu'allaq): A suspended divorce that takes effect only upon fulfillment of a specified condition.
استحاضة (istihadah): Irregular, non-menstrual bleeding that does not meet the definition of hayd (less than three days or more than ten).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The woman sees blood for one day
B) The woman sees blood for three continuous days
C) The woman misses a period
D) The woman completes her entire menstrual cycle

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a conditional divorce based on menstruation requires the woman to have blood flow continuously for three full days; shorter durations do not constitute hayd and do not trigger divorce.

Conditional Divorce on Menstruation: Purity Condition
Ruling on the timing of divorce when conditioned on a completed menstruation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for the occurrence of divorce when the husband uses the phrase 'haydah' and explain why complete purity is required.

Core Idea
Divorce conditional on a full menstruation only takes effect after the woman becomes completely pure from that period.

In this lesson, we examine a specific wording in conditional divorce: the husband's use of 'haydah' (a complete menstruation). The Hanafi school holds that the condition is not met until the entire menstrual period ends and the woman achieves purity. This distinguishes the ruling from cases where the condition is simply 'menstruation'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional phrase
The husband says 'اذا حضت حيضة فانت طالق'. This includes the word 'حيضة'.
2
Define 'haydah'
'Haydah' means a complete menstrual period, from beginning to end including subsequent purity. The term implies completion.
3
Apply the condition
For the condition to be fulfilled, the woman must experience a full haydah, which ends when she becomes pure (bleeding stops and she performs ghusl).
4
State the ruling
Therefore, divorce does not occur until the woman is pure from that menstruation.

Source Text

The Hanafi position holds that the condition is not fulfilled until the woman completes her menstrual period and becomes pure (i.e., the bleeding stops and she performs ghusl). Simply starting menstruation is insufficient; the entire hayd period must end.

Key Terms

Conditional Divorce on Fasting: Sunset Occasion
Ruling on conditional divorce with fasting condition.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
A conditional divorce linked to fasting occurs at sunset, not at dawn.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario in Hanafi divorce law: when a husband conditions divorce on his wife fasting a day. The ruling clarifies that the divorce is effective only upon completion of the fast, i.e., at sunset. Understanding this distinction is important because it determines the exact timing of the divorce and its legal consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
The husband's statement conditions divorce on the wife fasting a day. The condition is a future act that must be completed.
2
Determine the nature of the condition
Fasting is an act that spans from dawn to sunset. The condition is fulfilled only at the end of the act, not at the beginning.
3
Identify the moment of fulfillment
The fast is completed at sunset. Therefore, the condition is satisfied at that moment.
4
Apply the ruling
Divorce occurs automatically at sunset when the fast is completed, assuming all other conditions (valid intention, etc.) are met.

Source Text
